    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1396168-i-ve-got-a-copy-but-haven-t-read-it-yet">I&#39;ve got a copy but haven&#39;t read it yet . My question is why do editions of this range from 159 to over 300 pages?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1396168'><a id="like_count_community_question_1396168" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1396168?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F348.The_Door_Into_Summer">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1396168-i-ve-got-a-copy-but-haven-t-read-it-yet">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1396168-i-ve-got-a-copy-but-haven-t-read-it-yet">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/140348-zachary"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1182308446i/140348._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/140348-zachary">Zachary</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">When the book was written most writers made their living selling to magazines that published fiction. Paperback editions were sold at newstands and dr<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">When the book was written most writers made their living selling to magazines that published fiction. Paperback editions were sold at newstands and drug stores and so on. Writers in those days had a financial incentive to keep their work short because that's what worked best for the magazines and store spinner racks. Times changed and book stores came along. The price of paperbacks begin to rise and consumers preferred longer books to get more for their money. So publishers began to reformat popular old titles by increasing font size, adding additional material like forwards etc, and messing with the margins. The text itself remained the same, but consumers were more likely to buy it if it had 300 pages rather than 120. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_280570006" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/280570006" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/280570006?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451085740" href="/book/show/2050679.The_Door_into_Summer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280570006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1860393238811647075">An enjoyable SF story from a Grandmaster.<br/><br/>The novel's protagonist, Daniel B. Davis, was a precursor to Hugh Farnham and even Lazarus Long somewhat, though Long was introduced earlier in 1941's Methusaleh's Children. Actually, Davis (and others) are thinly disguised Heinlein: fiercely individualist, libertarian, technically savvy, hard working yet innovative, resourceful, wise cracking, and with a horn dog libido that would make a porn star blush. <br/><br/>I wonder if Door Into Summer used some of the same</span>
  <span id="freeText1860393238811647075" style="display:none">An enjoyable SF story from a Grandmaster.<br><br>The novel's protagonist, Daniel B. Davis, was a precursor to Hugh Farnham and even Lazarus Long somewhat, though Long was introduced earlier in 1941's Methusaleh's Children. Actually, Davis (and others) are thinly disguised Heinlein: fiercely individualist, libertarian, technically savvy, hard working yet innovative, resourceful, wise cracking, and with a horn dog libido that would make a porn star blush. <br><br>I wonder if Door Into Summer used some of the same notes and ideas that would later surface in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/353.Time_Enough_for_Love" title="Time Enough for Love by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Time Enough for Love</a>? Door into Summer may be classified as a transitional book between the earlier juvenile works and his middle works (his apex, his high water mark) and then to the weird, time traveling and lusty later books. I think a better reviewer than me could even make the point that Heinlein had begun his ascendancy here.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1577456642i/28675268.jpg" width="282" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1860393238811647075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3706874012981694935">I'm a little surprised I don't seem to have posted a review of this one before. I read this book "way back when". I probably read it first when I was in high school or just after. That would probably be the 1960s. I went through a period when I discovered Heinlein and ran through everything I could get my hands on by him. Some I didn't care for, some I liked and some I loved.<br/><br/>Many people place this in his so called "teen reads" but there is some question about that due to some of the subject matt</span>
  <span id="freeText3706874012981694935" style="display:none">I'm a little surprised I don't seem to have posted a review of this one before. I read this book "way back when". I probably read it first when I was in high school or just after. That would probably be the 1960s. I went through a period when I discovered Heinlein and ran through everything I could get my hands on by him. Some I didn't care for, some I liked and some I loved.<br /><br />Many people place this in his so called "teen reads" but there is some question about that due to some of the subject matter. Heinlein was very much a libertarian and had very open ideas about most things. This includes sex. Among some there's a bit of, consternation concerning the "final love interest" in the book.<br /><br />Just try it yourself and decide for yourself.<br /><br />Daniel Boone Davis (many of Heinlein's protagonists have names hearkening back to American history) is a master engineer and inventor...and that's what he wants to do. In the story which is placed in the "near future" (1970)...among other things in this "near future" people are able to take cold sleep. They can be frozen and awakened months or years later.<br /><br />Now, if I go any further in this synopsis it will involve spoilers so I'll simply suggest you try the book if for some reason you have managed to miss it. There's something here for hard science fiction fans, science fantasy fans, animal lovers (particularly cat lovers), and fans of just plain good books. I can and do recommend this one. Enjoy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3706874012981694935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3465799650" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3465799650" />
    <a title="Timothy Urges" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13015682-timothy-urges"><img alt="Timothy Urges" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563309359p2/13015682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3465799650?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy Urges" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy Urges" href="/user/show/13015682-timothy-urges">Timothy Urges</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3465799650&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3465799650" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6887716311014348796">Well, that was lame...and weird. I would have given this a 3, but that ending was hella strange. <br/><br/>Honestly, I couldn’t get into this book and was mostly bored the whole time. <br/><br/>Davis takes the “long sleep” in 1970, wakes up 30 years later, and nothing exciting comes of it other than needing to get a job and figure out what happened to his inventions and stocks. There is barely any discussion of the changes that occurred over the 30 year period. There is mention of a large amount of new jargon, but </span>
  <span id="freeText6887716311014348796" style="display:none">Well, that was lame...and weird. I would have given this a 3, but that ending was hella strange. <br /><br />Honestly, I couldn’t get into this book and was mostly bored the whole time. <br /><br />Davis takes the “long sleep” in 1970, wakes up 30 years later, and nothing exciting comes of it other than needing to get a job and figure out what happened to his inventions and stocks. There is barely any discussion of the changes that occurred over the 30 year period. There is mention of a large amount of new jargon, but we get one new term: “movies” are now “grabbies.” ?? And there are some robots. <br /><br />The plot is straight up silly. Davis sleeps 30 years, gets his inventions ripped off by his crazy con ex, realizes that he himself was the inventor and wasn’t actually ripped off, travels back in time to invent the things, then goes back to sleep for another 30 years to marry a little girl. <br /><br />Now let’s discuss the little girl. Ricky. Wtf was that? Davis knows Ricky as a child, he never meets her as an adult until she wakes from the long sleep, which he led her to do. Before he ever meets her as an adult, he wants to marry her. When she is 11 and finds out he will be asleep the next 30 years, she gets sad, decides/is persuaded to take the long sleep also, and asks him to marry her when she wakes up. He agrees and tells her that that’s the exact reason he is doing everything that he has done. ??? And he talks about how her breasts have not filled out yet and how she’s adorable. I tried to be objective about the situation but that was so weird to me. He only knows her as a child. I’m not offended by taboo topics, but wtf kind of goal is that for a main character? His literal goal is to marry her and that is the end of the book. <br /><br />This is a weird ass book that was too all over the place for me. I don’t know what to think about Heinlein. And this has an average score of 4 stars on Goodreads...why??</span>
  <a data-text-id="6887716311014348796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193268008?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193268008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7466420295696391479">I liked it, but it was suggested to me I shouldn't give four stars to every single book I enjoy, so here it goes for Heinlein. I really had no issues with "The Door Into Summer", and Heinlein is still one of my favorite SF masters after this.<br/><br/>I enjoy books that feature engineers as protagonists, and here we have one proto-geek singlehandedly inventing robotics in the 50's and failing rather spectacularly in the human relations department. Later on, there's some time travel thrown in and some cryo</span>
  <span id="freeText7466420295696391479" style="display:none">I liked it, but it was suggested to me I shouldn't give four stars to every single book I enjoy, so here it goes for Heinlein. I really had no issues with "The Door Into Summer", and Heinlein is still one of my favorite SF masters after this.<br /><br />I enjoy books that feature engineers as protagonists, and here we have one proto-geek singlehandedly inventing robotics in the 50's and failing rather spectacularly in the human relations department. Later on, there's some time travel thrown in and some cryogenics, giving us a glimpse of what the year 2000 would look like to a 1950 citizen. It's interesting to note how we surpassed some of Heinlein expectations in the field of artificial intelligence and miniaturization, and still didn't invent regenerating teeth, disposable shirts or beard removal cream. There's something very similar to velcro replacing zippers, but with a rather fancy field energy source.<br /><br />Heinlein prose is clean and fast, without his sometime annoying preaching and with some quality humor, courtesy of Petronius the Arbiter. Some of his sexual liberation stuff is included, like a nudist camp or an engagement with an 11 year old, but it doesn't take a central role to the story.<br /><br /><br />[edit for spelling 2015]</span>
  <a data-text-id="7466420295696391479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1239174428" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5986455447125337522">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1980/07/talking-lot-about-cats-door-into-summer.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/><br/>Talking a Lot About Cats: "A Door Into Summer" by Robert A. Heinlein<br/><br/><br/><br/>(Original Review, 1980-07-28)<br/><br/><br/><br/>Probably the biggest role for a cat outside of Norton is in Heinlein's A DOOR INTO SUMMER --- the hero talks more to his cat than he does to the woman he ends up marrying.<br/><br/> </span>
  <span id="freeText5986455447125337522" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1980/07/talking-lot-about-cats-door-into-summer.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Talking a Lot About Cats: "A Door Into Summer" by Robert A. <div id="review_314060309" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/314060309" />
    <a title="S.C. Jensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8843430-s-c-jensen"><img alt="S.C. Jensen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442970307p2/8843430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/314060309?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S.C. Jensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S.C. Jensen" href="/user/show/8843430-s-c-jensen">S.C. Jensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345330123" href="/book/show/786003.The_Door_into_Summer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer314060309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11236991110513863954">I didn't read this book with the intention of writing a review, so you'll excuse me if I don't go into great detail. Let me just summarize "the feel" of the book...<br/><br/>It is not very often that I read a book that makes me smile the entire time I'm reading it; this is one of them. From the hilarious anachronisms of the 1950's Futurist to the brilliant side-kick cat, Pete. (Cat lovers will appreciate this book on a completely different level than other readers). I was laughing out loud at least once e</span>
  <span id="freeText11236991110513863954" style="display:none">I didn't read this book with the intention of writing a review, so you'll excuse me if I don't go into great detail. Let me just summarize "the feel" of the book...<br /><br />It is not very often that I read a book that makes me smile the entire time I'm reading it; this is one of them. From the hilarious anachronisms of the 1950's Futurist to the brilliant side-kick cat, Pete. (Cat lovers will appreciate this book on a completely different level than other readers). I was laughing out loud at least once every 20 pages or so.<br /><br />It is only because I read some of the other reviews for this book that I felt the need to write a review myself. After seeing that a number of reviews that charge <i>The Door into Summer</i> (and sometimes Heinlein himself) as being both misogynistic and perverse, I felt the need to defend it (and him).<br /><br />First of all, on the complaints that Heinlein's vision of the future (from 1956, remember) is sexist, misogynistic, anti-woman, etc.:<br /><br />There are not many women in this story, true enough, which may be a mark against it in and of itself. Because of this, the heinous Belle stands out as being a particularly unlikable femme-fatale. Though I would argue that, had Belle not been foiled by Dan's foray into time travel, her plot would have succeeded and she would have made a respectable villain. She was well-equipped for it: calculating, edgy, violent, and un-emotional. But because the other women in the book (Jenny Sutton, the Girl-Scout Matron, and later Ricki) are fairly minor they do little to offset the influence of Belle and rather support the 1950's housewife stereotype. And Dan Davis' engineering vision of rescuing women from the drudgery of housework is a little dated, to be sure.<br /><br />However, I consider these to be the faults of a novel written in the 1950's. I always find it best to approach a book with the understanding that it is a product of the time in which it was written. If a novel breaks through the conventions of its time, great! But it would be unreasonable to expect it every time one picks up a new book. Our modern sensibilities might be offended by some archaic ideas, but out-dated notions don't necessarily devalue an otherwise good yarn. (not to mention historically important works)<br /><br />It's true that science fiction often pushes boundaries: of politics, religion, war, gender, sexuality, human nature, etc. But it is <u>not</u> necessary. And it is certainly not necessary to push all of them at once. <i>The Door into Summer</i> is not a book about gender roles. It reflects opinions common to the time in which it was written, but it does not address them specifically. It cannot be said to be particularly forward thinking on the subject, but at the same time it is a passive position. Heinlein is not actively or purposefully oppressing women in this novel, but he is describing a world very similar to the one in which he lived. Which, for me, is enough that I didn't hate the novel for its faults.<br /><br />Heinlein has shown in this and other novels that he is not rigid in his notions on the future of gender roles. In <i>Starship Troopers</i> women make the best fighter pilots because of their superior reflexes and mental dexterity. In this novel, there are suggestions that--outside of the narrative--women are fulfilling more diverse roles than we see them in. Dan Davis, when discussing the merits of his engineering robot 'Drafting Dan', admits that most women don't care much for it <u>unless they are engineers themselves</u>! The offhand nature of this remark is indicative that it is not an alien idea to Dan. Perhaps his housekeeping robot is more liberal-minded than we initially supposed, if it has freed women from the role of housewives to pursue their dreams outside the home. Something to consider, anyways.<br /><br />With that out of the way, I wanted to talk about the so-called perversion of Dan's unconventional (temporally speaking) romance with Ricki. Many people have commented on the "disturbing" nature of the love story sub-plot. And maybe it's because I've recently read <i>Lolita</i>, but I really didn't feel too put out about it. I actually found Dan and Ricki's relationship kind of cute, mostly because Dan falls in love with Ricki because she understands and appreciates his cat--which Dan feels is indicative of the kind of person she is (although she is only a child). It is important to note that there are no overtly pedophilic suggestions in this book, unless the reader supplies them (I'm sure there are those who will disagree)<br /><br />When it comes down to it, Dan's romantic feelings towards Ricki are not directed at her juvenile self but at the woman he imagines she will become. It is not unusual, I think, to idealize and idolize romantically (particularly after one has had ones heart broken). Ricki is the only female that Dan has ever felt any connection with, and he values her friendship. It is only after Belle betrays him that he begins to think "if only Ricki were older". Not because he fantasizes about being with a child (obviously, he wouldn't then wish she were older) but because he fantasizes about being with someone he loves and trusts.<br /><br />He cannot even be said to be taking advantage of her childish crush on him. He tells Ricki to wait until she's 20 to decide if she wants to be with him (he is, and will remain, 30). Ricki has 8 sobering years to decide if she still has feelings for Dan once she is an adult, during which he can supply no pressure. Thanks to the invention of suspended animation their love is possible without being creepy!<br /><br />Ok, so that's a longer rant than I intended. But there it is. Thanks for bearing with me if you got this far!<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="11236991110513863954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18418896" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18418896" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/322183-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218138040p2/322183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18418896?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/322183-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer18418896&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18418896" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7022642899210419629">Ultimately, creepy, and not in a good way. It's a time travel tale, and I'll forgive a lot for an entertaining time travel story. But "entertaining" and "time travel" are all it's got going for it. I haven't read a lot of Heinlein, but this didn't show me at all why he's got got so many fans. <br/><br/>The writing style is fine, but he goes wrong in a few key ways with the story. [SPOILERS] For one, he wrote the book in 1956, with most characters' natural time being 1970 and the rest of the action (reache</span>
  <span id="freeText7022642899210419629" style="display:none">Ultimately, creepy, and not in a good way. It's a time travel tale, and I'll forgive a lot for an entertaining time travel story. But "entertaining" and "time travel" are all it's got going for it. I haven't read a lot of Heinlein, but this didn't show me at all why he's got got so many fans. <br /><br />The writing style is fine, but he goes wrong in a few key ways with the story. [SPOILERS] For one, he wrote the book in 1956, with most characters' natural time being 1970 and the rest of the action (reached cryogenically and returned from via time travel) being in 2000 and 2001. Both are full of technological, cultural, and historical differences Heinlein invented. These probably seemed cool in 1957 or 1960, but his 2000 here wouldn't have held up in 1985, let alone 2008. <br /><br />Spoiling with age as badly as his inaccurate visions of change is his vision of something wholly unchanged over 50 years: a degree of sexism astonishing these days. Hell, I suspect his degree of sexism would have been startling even in the real 1970. It's not constant and overpowering; it's just striking.<br /><br />Each of those would be flaws I wouldn't mind having to overlook for the sake of the time travel story, which is largely entertaining, but Heinlein completely undermines any satisfaction that could come from it by having the protagonist and narrator, Dan Davis, eagerly find a way (via the rampant cryogenics of imaginary 1970) to marry in 2000 the step-daughter of his business partner, a girl who, as a child called him "Uncle Danny." <br /><br />He'd known her since she was a toddler or something (in 1970, she's nine and he's [always] thirty), and there's talk early in the book about how she had a crush on him and wanted to marry him when she grew up, but it's just some misplaced childhood crush and, of course, nothing he actually reciprocates. Supposedly. Ultimately, though, after all the time travel stuff (which you could totally separate pretty easily from this weirdness), during which he makes various occasional references to how great this kid is and how important she is to him, after he returns from 2000 (during which time he never met her as an adult) to 1970 and takes care of fun paradox stuff, he tells her that he's going to go into thirty-year cryostasis. (Again, though no one but he knows it's his 2nd time.) 30 years seems like forever to a young kid, and she's upset she won't see him again, and he tells her that in 1982, when she's 21, she could do "The Long Sleep" too and come out of it also in 2000. She asks, "If I do...will you marry me?" He replies, damn disturbingly, "Yes....That's what I want. That's why I'm doing this." <br /><br />After they awake in 2000, they get married pretty much immediately. And then the book ends within a few pages.<br /><br />So Heinlein's a freak and a perv. I don't care that much that this girl is physically 21 when they hook up. The "hero" never knew her as an adult, just as a young kid who called him "Uncle Danny," and it was based on that relationship that he decided he wanted to marry her. Also, the 21-year-old who opted for the cryogenics in 1982 apparently still has the precise same feelings as her nine-year-old self, which is similarly disturbing. Goddamn. Fricking. Creepy. <br /><br />Throughout the book, the protagonist (and narrator) doesn't seem like a really great guy but sympathetic enough. If Heinlein's trying to blow the reader's mind by having him turn out to be not at all sympathetic after all that, I could see the point in that. But there's no indication that's what the author's up to or, indeed, that he sees anything wrong here. It seems instead that he's glad for Dan to have a happy ending. Parents of 1957, keep your children away from Robert Heinlein, please.<br /><br />My default tendency is to react favorably to time travel fiction, so it's pretty striking to make that U-turn from amusement to revulsion there at the end and move this from the "OK" column into "Not OK. Not."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7022642899210419629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_423592357" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423592357" />
    <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield"><img alt="Lance Greenfield" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658682p2/2793431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423592357?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lance Greenfield" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield">Lance Greenfield</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345330123" href="/book/show/786003.The_Door_into_Summer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2793431-lance-greenfield?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423592357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16080855073456493749">I really enjoyed this book from beginning to [almost] end. The reason for the "almost" will become apparent. <br/><br/>The story of time travel by various means was excellent. When reading this story, you should remember that it was written in the 1950s. Some of Heinlein's predictions are amazing, and some are way off the mark. It's amazing to follow his line of thinking though.<br/><br/>You can see an outline of the plot in the description. It is fairly predictable, but that didn't detract from my enjoyment of the</span>
  <span id="freeText16080855073456493749" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book from beginning to [almost] end. The reason for the "almost" will become apparent. <br /><br />The story of time travel by various means was excellent. When reading this story, you should remember that it was written in the 1950s. Some of Heinlein's predictions are amazing, and some are way off the mark. It's amazing to follow his line of thinking though.<br /><br />You can see an outline of the plot in the description. It is fairly predictable, but that didn't detract from my enjoyment of the story at all. It was fun, and it was refreshing to read such innocent prose.<br /><br />Although some people might be offended, there was some narrative that would be considered non-PC these days, but was just part of life in the 'fifties. I actually found that quite refreshing too. I get so irritated by the over-sensitivity to political correctness these days. You can't even tell a good Irish joke, or drop your pants in a US bar to proudly show off your British tattoo, these days, without drawing comments from the puritans.<br /><br />The story was great right up until the final chapter. This was a bit of a damp squib, Heinlein felt that his hero had to justify and explain his actions and how several instances of himself could coexist. I would have been far more satisfied with the explosive ending which could so easily have been there.<br /><br />Having said that, I would strongly recommend this book to all lovers of time travel and sci-fi books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16080855073456493749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1724557860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7294920471577965933">Are you a cat person? If yes, you’re definitely in for a treat. Pete is an amazingly written cat character if there ever was one. Yes, cat lovers will surely appreciate this one. Cat owners will understand that cats like Pete are as rare as true love and only come once in a life time, if we’re lucky, hence they will understand what makes Pete so special to Dan, his owner. However, even if you happen to hate cats, I’m sure you’ll appreciate the ingenious way Pete is described. That’s just plain g</span>
  <span id="freeText7294920471577965933" style="display:none">Are you a cat person? If yes, you’re definitely in for a treat. Pete is an amazingly written cat character if there ever was one. Yes, cat lovers will surely appreciate this one. Cat owners will understand that cats like Pete are as rare as true love and only come once in a life time, if we’re lucky, hence they will understand what makes Pete so special to Dan, his owner. However, even if you happen to hate cats, I’m sure you’ll appreciate the ingenious way Pete is described. That’s just plain good writing. <br /><br />I’m making it sound like this is a novel about cat. Well, it’s not but it sorts of is. The cat I’m talking about is an important character, not in any futuristic genetically altered pet sense of the world, he just happens to be a side kick of our protagonist. Who might that be? Our protagonist is Dan Davis, an engineer, a well written characters, one could say a typical Heinlein hero. You know, a very intelligent badass guy who is on to take on the world and won’t let anything stops him. He is charming yet bold, brave but thoughtful, no fool but willing to take calculated risks.<br /><br />Nevertheless, this isn’t exactly how he starts out! When the novel opens, he is not up to much. The novel opens with our guy drinking himself to death on account of his best friend and his ex-finance tricking him out of his invention (and hard earned future glory as well as financial security). So, he is a bit depressed. The only person he cares for in this world is Ricky, who happens to be a ten-year-old daughter of his late friend. He respects her and we suppose he imagines she might be a good companion someday because he decided to freeze himself so that they can be together in ten years’ time.<br /><br /> Some people find this part to be quite disturbing, but it isn’t like he wants to be with her when she’s a kid, he is romantically interested in the women she will be once she grows up. There is no knowing who that will be, you might say and yes that would be a valid point but it doesn’t seem to bother our protagonist. If Pete likes her, the girl must be ok and will stay that way (maybe Dan believes in cat’s intuition). I would say there is nothing fishy in the relationship between Dan and Ricky, but I can see why some might consider it to be a bit weird. Why the need to freeze himself up in the first place? He could have waited for her to grow up, but that sounds creepy doesn’t it? To watch someone growing up and then to marry them? Way creepier than just freezing yourself up and meeting them in the future? Actually yes.<br /><br />It is interesting that he wants this relationship to happen when they’re about the same age. Does that indicate that he actually thinks there is something wrong in relationships in which there exists a significant age difference? Or more likely he needs the freezing up part to make the story more interesting? I mean this is a SF novel and we’re all waiting for the story to become well more SF. Furthermore, you know Dan is not motivated just by the desire to be with the future grown up Ricky. There isn’t much talk about Ricky if you think about it, this isn’t Lolita. There is not much talk about his feelings at that point and that’s not going to change drastically. Don’t expect him to get all emotional, it is not going to happen. This avoidance of emotional talk (or perhaps even thoughts) may be due to the fact that Dan makes that freezing himself up decision after a long period of drinking. It may be that his true feelings for Ricky only develop later on. At any rate, she isn’t the sole motivation.<br /><br />Dan wants revenge. He wants to rub it into the face of his ex. He wants to be young when she is old. Perhaps most importantly, he wants to live in the future. He is an engineer you know. He is wondering what the world will look like in a number of years. Speaking of which, it is fascinating to observe how correct or how wrong Heinlein’s predictions were. There is certainly a lot of talk about the future in this one. Moreover, there is the actual ‘future’ experienced by a man from another time epoch and you can trust in one of the greats of SF to make that part good. <br />It does get more interesting because things don’t go as planned, I mean the freezing up and all. When he wakes up in the future, he starts seeing things he is sure he had himself invented. As he said, every engineer has his own signature and it is this signature he is seeing everywhere that is driving him crazy.<br /><br />Enters time travel. You know a book about just being frozen wasn’t going to cut it. He needs to travel back in time. There needs to be more action. Not surprisingly, that is what we get. More action and adventure. No complains here. You know I’m not even sure if women play an important part in this novel. Perhaps not really. We have one typical femme-fatale and one angelic girl, but the relationships with them are never explored into great detail. As far as relationships are concerned, this is more about the love between a man and his cat then about love between a man and a woman. Just for a record, I don’t have a problem with that. Not everything has to be all romantic and stuff.<br /><br />There is certainly a way to read the story between Dan and Ricky that makes it all sweet and romantic, but I think Ricky is there mostly for the plot. This is a one-man show or more precisely one man and his cat show! Romance isn’t a fundamental part of it, but it doesn’t feel out of place either. As I said, the relationship between Dan and Ricky can be read in a very sweet light (or in a slightly creepy one) …or it can be just ignored, if that isn’t your thing. <br /><br /> The Door into Summer is a very good SF novel that is mostly about adventure, time travel, engineering, future predictions, action, being badass and all those things we love about Heinlein. It is interesting enough to keep us intellectually stimulated and there is a touch of emotion that is just enough. I really enjoyed reading it. I have a copy at home, so I’ll probably reread it again eventually (emphasis on again because I already reread it). I recommend this to engineers, Heinlein’s fans, cat’s lovers, readers of SF as well as those interested in time travel stories. This is a fabulously written SF novel, pretty short but filled with such lovely things! The protagonist is and awfully charming and smart guy and it is great fun reading about his adventures. If you want to read a novel with a guy that makes it against all odds, this is the novel for you.<br /><br />Final words? The Door Into Summer is a highly enjoyable read. It is not Heinlein’s best, but it is a very good novel. It found it to be incredibly uplifting and fun. In addition, this novel managed to accomplish what many great works of literature have tried but failed and that is describe perfectly an infinitely complex creature called....the cat.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7294920471577965933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_582265570" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/582265570" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/582265570?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345330123" href="/book/show/786003.The_Door_into_Summer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=satirical-humorous">satirical-humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=classic-sf">classic-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer582265570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1689737813828426839"><b>The Door Into Summer: A charming time-travel story from Golden Age Heinlein</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a><br/>The Door Into Summer (1957) is an immensely enjoyable time-travel story told effortlessly by Robert A. Heinlein long before he turned into a crotchety, soap-box ranting old crank who had a very unhealthy obsession with free love and characters going back in time to hook up with their mothers (gross!!). <br/><br/>So back to this book. It’s the story of Daniel Davis, a hard-working engineer in 1</span>
  <span id="freeText1689737813828426839" style="display:none"><b>The Door Into Summer: A charming time-travel story from Golden Age Heinlein</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a><br />The Door Into Summer (1957) is an immensely enjoyable time-travel story told effortlessly by Robert A. Heinlein long before he turned into a crotchety, soap-box ranting old crank who had a very unhealthy obsession with free love and characters going back in time to hook up with their mothers (gross!!). <br /><br />So back to this book. It’s the story of Daniel Davis, a hard-working engineer in 1970 who invents a wonderful robot vacuum cleaner named Hired Girl (not at all sexist, right?), but has more ambitious plans for an all-purpose household robot called Flexible Frank. He collaborates with his business partner Miles Gentry and assistant named Belle Darkin. However, one evening Dan discovers that his partner Miles is in cahoots with Belle to wrest control of the company from him. They take a controlling share and fire him as Chief Engineer, and to make matters worse they steal his designs for Flexible Frank. He is so upset that he elects to go into “cold sleep”, entrusting his stock certificates to Ricky, the stepdaughter of Miles, hoping to wake up to a better world in 2000.<br /><br />Of course when he is revived all is not well. His plan has not worked, the company that Miles and Belle ran has gone bankrupt, and a different company seems to have developed Flexible Frank under the name of Eager Beaver. Dan is at a loss to figure out what has happened. He starts to follow a series of clues that point to a number of paradoxes that could only be explained by time travel…<br /><br />Hang on, did I forget to mention the most important character in the story? Indeed I did, for the most charming figure in the book is a tomcat named Petronius the Arbiter (Pete for short), and he really steals the show. Dan brings Pete everywhere, including to restaurants and bars, where he keeps him hidden in a bag but orders him drinks. Pete plays an absolutely critical role later in the story, but Heinlein’s descriptions of Pete should really resonate with cat lovers.<br /><br />Upon further reflection, I may have to revise my earlier statement that Heinlein didn’t delve into any of his later creepy obsessions about women or mothers. In this story the little girl Ricky is a plucky kid who is wise beyond her years, and Dan really admires her, imagining what a fine young woman she might grow up to be. But wait, if he goes into “cold sleep” for 30 years, won’t that bring their ages closer together? Actually it’s much more complicated than that, and why bother getting together with Ricky when she’s in her 40s when you can manage things so she is only 21 instead? How is this possible? Well, when you’re the author you can make anything happen, didn’t you know?<br /><br />So lurking under the surface of this otherwise charming and very cleverly-constructed time-travel story, we have yet another subtext of creepy wish fulfillment. It really didn’t have to be part of the story, but then again this is Heinlein, and for him writing was always an opportunity to explore his own fantasies and political ideas. If you can overlook this, and it’s such a brisk and well-told story, I think you will find it quite enjoyable, even if he is laying the foundations for later travesties like Time Enough for Love, To Sail Beyond the Sunset, and The Number of the Beast.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1689737813828426839" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_619785031" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/619785031" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/695116-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340192527p2/695116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/619785031?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780786176922" href="/book/show/50885.The_Door_Into_Summer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer619785031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1991334347782940009">Another old favorite picked up as a downloadable audio book from the library. It was quite enjoyable in this medium & the reader was very good. Originally published in 1957, it is set in 'the future' years 1970 & 2000. The idea of traveling into the future via 'cold sleep' was a pretty popular until sometime in the 70's, but cutting edge at this time, I think. Haven't heard about it in humans for years.<br/><br/>The hero, Dan, is an engineer & inventor. His genius isn't in break through technology, but in</span>
  <span id="freeText1991334347782940009" style="display:none">Another old favorite picked up as a downloadable audio book from the library. It was quite enjoyable in this medium &amp; the reader was very good. Originally published in 1957, it is set in 'the future' years 1970 &amp; 2000. The idea of traveling into the future via 'cold sleep' was a pretty popular until sometime in the 70's, but cutting edge at this time, I think. Haven't heard about it in humans for years.<br /><br />The hero, Dan, is an engineer &amp; inventor. His genius isn't in break through technology, but in putting together mostly off-the-shelf parts to create really useful laborsaving devices. Steve Jobs type genius, timing, &amp; design. Heinlein's discussion of this tech timing over the course of the book is very practical &amp; interesting. It's amazing how much supporting technology there has to be for every major breakthrough.<br /><br /><i>Engineering is the art of the practical and depends more on the total state of the art than it does on the individual engineer. When railroading time comes you can railroad-but not before. Look at poor Professor Langley, breaking his heart on a flying machine that should have flown-he had put the necessary genius in it-but he was just a few years too early to enjoy the benefit of collateral art he needed and did not have. Or take the great Leonardo daVinci, so far out of his time that his most brilliant concepts were utterly unbuildable.</i><br /><br />It was Heinlein's genius to take this a step further into the prosaic &amp; make it sound so easy &amp; obvious.<br /><br /><i>Amazingly little real thought had been given to housework, even though it is at least 50 per cent of all work in the world. The women's magazines talked about "labor saving in the home" and "functional kitchens," but it was just prattle; their pretty pictures showed living-working arrangements essentially no better than those in Shakespeare's day; the horse-to-jet-plane revolution had not reached the home.</i><br /><br />Of course, Heinlein got a lot wrong about the future, but that wasn't too bad. Most obviously, we still don't have most of the devices that he describes. I loved his idea of Thorsen Memory tubes &amp; macro programming, even though both are silly &amp; simplistic. He had helicopter buses &amp; completely missed the idea of the Internet - overall communications or electronic databases - yet he had transmutation of elements. Not a bad reason, if incorrect, for getting off the gold standard &amp; he had the timing pretty close.<br /><br />The overall story was a pretty good one of love &amp; betrayal. With the time travel tossed in, it got quite twisty - although I was a little disappointed the he seemed to try to obscure it a bit too much especially in the last conversation. That was too much as the character is supposed to be fairly intelligent. <br /><br />And that brings me to the creep factor that really brings the book down for me - Dan's relationship with Ricky. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ What do you call a guy who promises to marry a 10 year old when she grows up &amp; then does it without even knowing her as an adult? Seriously, he did &amp; the scene was so poorly done, too. Ricky talks like the little girl she is yet Dan treats her like the full grown woman she will be. It was seriously icky. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />So it was a 4 star story with 1 star removed due to this one creepy factor. It's well worth reading or listening too, though. Glad I did again after all these years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1991334347782940009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1486225934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1486225934" />
    <a title="Andreas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20379307-andreas"><img alt="Andreas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512938270p2/20379307.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1486225934?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andreas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andreas" href="/user/show/20379307-andreas">Andreas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20379307-andreas?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20379307-andreas?shelf=classic-sff">classic-sff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1486225934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16469613668542374488">I liked it far less than my previous RAH reads of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16690.The_Moon_is_a_Harsh_Mistress' title='The Moon is a Harsh Mistress by Robert A. Heinlein' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17214.Starship_Troopers' title='Starship Troopers by Robert A. Heinlein' rel='nofollow noopener'>Starship Troopers</a>, or even <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/350.Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land' title='Stranger in a Strange Land by Robert A. Heinlein' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stranger in a Strange Land</a>.<br/><br/>The story - silly technician looses his garage corporation predictably to greedy woman and former business partner - wasn't very good with all that implausible back and forth through time and hibernated sleep. RAH rode that SF trope but didn't motivate it well enough; a lot of less riskier and far easier solutions to the protagonist's problems lay on hands. Especi</span>
  <span id="freeText16469613668542374488" style="display:none">I liked it far less than my previous RAH reads of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16690.The_Moon_is_a_Harsh_Mistress" title="The Moon is a Harsh Mistress by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17214.Starship_Troopers" title="Starship Troopers by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Starship Troopers</a>, or even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/350.Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land" title="Stranger in a Strange Land by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Stranger in a Strange Land</a>.<br /><br />The story - silly technician looses his garage corporation predictably to greedy woman and former business partner - wasn't very good with all that implausible back and forth through time and hibernated sleep. RAH rode that SF trope but didn't motivate it well enough; a lot of less riskier and far easier solutions to the protagonist's problems lay on hands. Especially his second hibernation was ridiculous. <br />Some of his sexual liberation stuff was needlessly included, especially the nudist camp, and I gnaw on those hints with the 11 year old girl.<br /><br />As SF, this one didn't transport well into our times - which is a sharp contrast to other works from RAH, e.g. Starship Troopers, mainly because of his sloppy world-building with implausible anchoring of technology in society.<br />Concerning computer technology, he wasn't at the scientific height of the time of his writing, e.g. he didn't mention high-level programming languages (plan calculus or FORTRAN); magnetic core memory was well established, even the first mass produced computers were available with the IBM 650. I'm a sucker for nostalgic views at computers, and I think it would make perfect sense to visit a computer museum alongside reading this novel. As it goes, the inventor of computers, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konrad_Zuse" rel="nofollow noopener">Konrad Zuse</a>, had his labs some 10 miles from my home and the community founded a <a>museum</a> there - sorry, folks, its all in German. Fascinating stuff!<br /><br />I understand that hibernation was a thing back in the 50s but I didn't like RAH's discussion of managing the legal parts. And a 70% survival chance would be an absolute no-go for me.<br /><br />Lots of his extrapolations of technology of the years 1970 (which was 14 years near-SF at that time) and 2000 were funny to read. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humanoid_robot" rel="nofollow noopener">Humanoid roboters</a> are a thing in this novel and some of them start to come true in our days, e.g. half-autonomous cars or cleaning roboters. It is interesting that it is far more complex to get legal issues cleared than getting the technology working, but RAH didn't dive into that one enough. Speaking of it, I like the concept of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pepper_(robot)" rel="nofollow noopener">Pepper</a> who is designed to "read emotions".<br /><br />But really devastating were his social and political predictions of the world's state of 2000. Only thrown-in were facts as "England as a Canadian colony" or constructs like "Greater L.A.", a "6 week war" or a French monarchy. Why, oh why? It would have been very interesting to find out motivations for this settings, but RAH concentrated more on his time travel and hibernation roundtrips. Which I didn't like.<br /><br />Sorry, only 2 stars - 1 of those for Pete the cat which I found quite funny and realistic as a character but very strange for a SF story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16469613668542374488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_169182461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169182461" />
    <a title="Derek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5420722-derek"><img alt="Derek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484231855p2/5420722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169182461?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek" href="/user/show/5420722-derek">Derek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5420722-derek?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5420722-derek?shelf=chaos-treasure-hunt">chaos-treasure-hunt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5420722-derek?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169182461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6233538501505823511"><div>
  <div>
    <div>I first read this many years ago—probably about the time in which it is set: it was published in 1957 (just before I was born) but most of the story is set in 1970 and the rest in 2000/2001.  The only thing that really stayed in my memory was the reason for the title. </div>
    <div>
      <br/>
    </div>
    <div>Dan Davis once lived in Connecticut in a house with twelve doors to the outside. In Winter, his cat Pete (Petronius the Arbiter) would make him open every door, looking for the one that led to Summer. </div></div></div></span>
  <span id="freeText6233538501505823511" style="display:none"><div>
  <div>
    <div>I first read this many years ago—probably about the time in which it is set: it was published in 1957 (just before I was born) but most of the story is set in 1970 and the rest in 2000/2001.  The only thing that really stayed in my memory was the reason for the title. </div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>Dan Davis once lived in Connecticut in a house with twelve doors to the outside. In Winter, his cat Pete (Petronius the Arbiter) would make him open every door, looking for the one that led to Summer. Pete's not present for the majority of the novel, but he's very definitely a major character.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>I pretty much stopped reading Heinlein after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/353.Time_Enough_for_Love" title="Time Enough for Love by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Time Enough for Love</a>. He got increasingly misogynistic and right-wing (or else, he'd always been that way and just felt he could get away with writing about it in his old age). But I'd forgotten the immense vision he brought to his earlier stories like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13099926.The_Roads_Must_Roll" title="The Roads Must Roll by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Roads Must Roll</a>, the very first Heinlein I read, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/121586.Waldo_and_Magic__Inc" title="Waldo and Magic, Inc by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Waldo and Magic, Inc</a>, and this one. My first introduction to Science Fiction was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7779.Arthur_C__Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, and Heinlein was my second. They share that vision of the possibilities of the future, and Clarke may actually have been technically more capable (when Clarke suggested satellites in Earth-orbit, I suspect he could have built one, with help—when Heinlein builds a general household robot he's imagining what we would want to have done, and the way it should operate, but I can't imagine he actually could have designed the necessary circuit boards), but Heinlein is far and above the better story-teller.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>Like many futurists, Heinlein's 50s vision of 1970 was a little too optimistic, and his vision of 2000 was much too optimistic, but still he wrote about so many things that have come to pass almost as he described. It's so stunningly accurate that the few anachronisms that creep in are totally hilarious.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>"For my money Chuck was the only real engineer there; the rest were overeducated slipstick mechanics."  Looking back from 60 years into Heinlein's future, it's hard to imagine that anyone would have missed the fact that "slipsticks" (slide rules) would be non-existent in 2000, and were on their way out in the 70s (I learned to use a slide rule in the early 70s, bought a beautiful one in 1977—at a huge discount—and have probably not seen one for sale since).</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>In 2001: "The nearest twenty-four-hour bank was downtown at the Grand Circle of the Ways." I actually remember when there were less than a handful of bank machines in the whole of Toronto (~1981), but in a novel that centres on the life of an engineer who specializes in automatons, it's funny that he never imagined we could do away with physical banks for the mere dispensing of money.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>But those things don't detract in the slightest from the things he got right (if not necessarily pinning them to the right time). Heinlein goes into great detail describing "Drafting Dan"—a way to automate drafting, so that an engineer can design without hunching over a drafting table. And what he describes is pretty much AutoCAD, only about a decade and a half too early.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>He describes Roombas. He places them nearly three decades too early, but the physical description of the way they will ensure that a whole room is vacuumed and then return to their charging stations is uncanny.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>One thing he got wrong, but it just goes even further to demonstrate his vision. In 2000, he postulates that, for some reason, gold has become very cheap. This leads to a great deal more automation, because all his robots need a lot of gold (perhaps not individually, but certainly in total) and with higher gold prices it becomes cost prohibitive. The prices of gold, platinum, and numerous other metals do in fact currently limit a great deal of our technology.</div>
    <div>
      <br />
    </div>
    <div>I recently finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/624122.The_Man_Who_Folded_Himself" title="The Man Who Folded Himself by David Gerrold" rel="nofollow noopener">The Man who Folded Himself</a>, a time travel story that's all about paradox. Heinlein takes a different view (and one that, failing actual experimentation, must be just as likely): <i>'But I'm not worried about "paradoxes" or "causing anachronisms"—if a thirtieth-century engineer does smooth out the bugs and then sets up transfer stations and trade, it will be because the Builder designed the universe that way…. He doesn't need busybodies to "enforce" His laws; they enforce themselves. There are no miracles and the word "anachronism" is a semantic blank.' </i> Heinlein's idea of time travel is that you can't do anything that you haven't already done. <i>"Free will and predestination in one sentence and both true"</i>. I've been trying to wrap my head around this idea, possibly even before I first read this story: I remember arguing with Calvinists as a teenager, who insisted that everything was predestined, but that we still had complete free will.  It's actually easier to believe in time paradoxes! </div>
  </div>
  <div>
    <br />
  </div>
  <div>Anyway, this particular story probably doesn't deserve the 5-star rating. I use that for life-changing books, and in Heinlein's case, that is probably <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13099926.The_Roads_Must_Roll" title="The Roads Must Roll by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Roads Must Roll</a>, but somewhere over the decades I lost that book so I can't reread it unless  I find another copy. This one certainly has similarities and can stand in until I find another copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13099926.The_Roads_Must_Roll" title="The Roads Must Roll by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Roads Must Roll</a>!</div>
</div></span>
  <a data-text-id="6233538501505823511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3433420553" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3433420553" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571965612p2/53587416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3433420553?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3433420553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12352019690251957845">An amusing, lighthearted time travel romp, with a particular appeal for cat lovers. I don't know there was much here of real interest, apart from some of Heinlein's musings in the last few pages on the nature of free will, determinism and the existence, or not, of multiple universes. Also intriguing was his notion that the laws of the universe are capable of enforcing themselves, without any help needed from us humans, giving us a kind of free pass from worrying about paradoxes and the like resu</span>
  <span id="freeText12352019690251957845" style="display:none">An amusing, lighthearted time travel romp, with a particular appeal for cat lovers. I don't know there was much here of real interest, apart from some of Heinlein's musings in the last few pages on the nature of free will, determinism and the existence, or not, of multiple universes. Also intriguing was his notion that the laws of the universe are capable of enforcing themselves, without any help needed from us humans, giving us a kind of free pass from worrying about paradoxes and the like resulting from time travel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12352019690251957845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2348828820079044174"><br/>Three and a half stars, so I guess my four stars will stand. <br/><br/>I read this as a teenager and have always remembered it as a love story. And I still think that. The difference is, this time I'm certain that the "love story" in this book is between narrator Dan and his cat, Pete, and definitely not between Dan and <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Frederica/Ricky (because there's a good reason why Ricky rhymes with squicky) <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The devotion and concern that Dan shows for his cat across time and distance i</span>
  <span id="freeText2348828820079044174" style="display:none"><br />Three and a half stars, so I guess my four stars will stand. <br /><br />I read this as a teenager and have always remembered it as a love story. And I still think that. The difference is, this time I'm certain that the "love story" in this book is between narrator Dan and his cat, Pete, and definitely not between Dan and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Frederica/Ricky (because there's a good reason why Ricky rhymes with squicky) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The devotion and concern that Dan shows for his cat across time and distance is very touching, and is what really makes this whole book. <br /><br />The most jarring bits of this for me was that it was written in 1957 and set in 1970 and 2001 -- and 2001 in this book is definitely an alternate universe, looking at it from 2015. <br /><br />An expanded review may follow. But in a nutshell, this held up much better as a reread than I was afraid it might, and I recommend it if you enjoy science fiction and/or books with a strong undercurrent of cat love. <br /><br />--------------------------------<br /><br />And excuse me, Goodreads, but the description of this book posted here is a huge spoiler that basically tells the whole plot! Someone should fix that. </span>
